    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer3569184881577610562">SPARK OF LIFE<br/><br/>509 is a political prisoner in a German concentration camp. For ten years, he has persevered in the most hellish conditions. Deathly weak, he still has his wits about him and he senses that the end of the war is near. If he and the other living corpses in his barracks can hold on for liberation--or force their own--then their suffering will not have been in va</span>
  <span id="freeText3569184881577610562" style="display:none">SPARK OF LIFE<br /><br />509 is a political prisoner in a German concentration camp. For ten years, he has persevered in the most hellish conditions. Deathly weak, he still has his wits about him and he senses that the end of the war is near. If he and the other living corpses in his barracks can hold on for liberation--or force their own--then their suffering will not have been in vain.<br /><br />Now the SS who run the camp are ratcheting up the terror. But their expectations are jaded and their defenses are down. It is possible that the courageous, yet terribly weak prisoners have just enough left in them to resist. And if they die fighting, they will die on their own terms, cheating the Nazis out of their devil's contract.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2311476914" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2311476914" />
    <a title="Lee Klein " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein"><img alt="Lee Klein " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594811741p2/90786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2311476914?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Klein " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Klein " href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein">Lee Klein </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2311476914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16379864712423928043">EMR dedicates this to his sister who was beheaded in a concentration camp, in part to reach her famous brother who had condemned the Nazis while in exile in Switzerland. Yet the author's presence isn't felt. It's never vindictive or violently passionate. The narrative voice never dehumanizes, never does unto others as others have done unto his sister -- it keeps its cool and holds the gaze of the slowly dying sado-ubermencsh until his eyes turn to sightless jelly. <br/><br/>A goddamn great novel, generall</span>
  <span id="freeText16379864712423928043" style="display:none">EMR dedicates this to his sister who was beheaded in a concentration camp, in part to reach her famous brother who had condemned the Nazis while in exile in Switzerland. Yet the author's presence isn't felt. It's never vindictive or violently passionate. The narrative voice never dehumanizes, never does unto others as others have done unto his sister -- it keeps its cool and holds the gaze of the slowly dying sado-ubermencsh until his eyes turn to sightless jelly. <br /><br />A goddamn great novel, generally. Rising drama for the win. Nearly as vivid, moving, significant, and unputdownable as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2302257413?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">A Time to Love and a Time to Die</a>, which I read directly before this one, which EMR wrote directly after this one. Both are set in Germany during WWII, both structured absolutely linearly, and to extraordinary effect both use the Allied bombings of civilian populations in smaller towns to propel them forward. <br /><br />"A Time to Love and A Time to Die" is more of a love story, a close-third POV limited to a single German soldier on leave -- like EMR knew he had something good with the Allied bombings but wanted to zoom-in on its effect on a single story/character after this one's roving POV, accessing the thoughts of dozens of prisoners, kapos, guards, SS, and townsfolk, although it primarily focuses on a prisoner known only as 509 for three quarters of the novel and the SS concentration camp commander, a former postal clerk. The camp isn't a death camp although it has a crematorium to dispose of bodies that waste away or are beaten to death. Some of the prisoners have been there more than a decade -- political prisoners, Communists, the editor of a newspaper who denounced the Nazis, a doctor who knew an SS officer had syphilis, etc. In the camp, the Communists and more democratic leaning prisoners have formed an underground coalition that meets and makes plans, gathers weapons, resists when it cans. <br /><br />At first it took 75 to 100 pages for the characters to cohere and the story to start when 509 and another character refuse to submit to the camp doctor's experiments -- they're beaten viciously but survive -- and since they're not executed they give the other prisoners hope. A major positive for this is its setting in time: late-winter/early spring 1945. As the weather warms and they hear artillery fire in the distance they know that they have weeks, maybe even days until liberation, so they do whatever they can to stall their deaths, to live just a few more days, to delay going up through the crematorium chimney. As the British and Americans approach, the novel takes off and I read it compulsively, wanting to return to it whenever I put it down. Who would survive after so many years in the camp? Man, I hope 509 makes it! Would the SS ship everyone off to a death camp or just murder all the prisoners, set the barracks on fire and machine gun them before abandoning the camp and running from the Allies? Or maybe the Allies would inadvertently bomb the camp?<br /><br />I won't divulge what happens but generally the ending makes it a great novel, especially for the depiction of the camp commander Neubauer who toward the end distances himself from real sadistic Nazis and tries to whitewash his reputation by planting some primroses around the barracks and increasing the food ration as he daydreams about surrendering as a military officer, ceremonially handing over his sword and informing his British and American conquerors that he had only been following orders and performing his duties, which as soldiers they will of course understand . . .<br /><br />4.75 stars rounded down for the occasional murky machinations of the prisoners' schemes (bartering, bribes, etc), some difficulty keeping the characters straight (similar names of a few prisoners may be intentional since after a while all the prisoners look like walking skeletons with huge eyes and nostrils too large), and a translation that seemed less artful, patient, and/or milky than the other EMR's I've read. The language in this seemed weaker to me, comparatively spare, quick/rushed (probably loyal to the text due to the POV), and occasional sentences in dialogue that end with the odd "what?" This "what?" threw me off every time it appeared -- I suppose it could be rendered in common American English as "huh?" Also a few errors in the text ("I" instead of "It" for example). <br /><br />But enough quibbling over minor demerits! Again, this is a great novel, a masterpiece of conventional rising drama, vivid, gripping, horrifying, never cartoonish, with moments of top-notch abstraction without getting in the way of the story's steady, natural progression. <br /><br />I'll definitely be an EMR completist by the end of the year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16379864712423928043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1042274487" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1042274487" />
    <a title="Hamish Davidson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13816232-hamish-davidson"><img alt="Hamish Davidson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578048754p2/13816232.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1042274487?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hamish Davidson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hamish Davidson" href="/user/show/13816232-hamish-davidson">Hamish Davidson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=prison">prison</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1042274487" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15940378028672879459">I am becoming a huge Remarque fan. Spark of Life is thoughtful and powerful writing. The story illustrates the enormity of the tragedy we know as the holocaust and if you want to be moved by a book, that will happen when you read this one for sure. A sad tale and a wake up call in one amazingly well written novel. The story is even sadder with the knowledge that the author's sister Elfreide, to whom the book is dedicated, was beheaded by the nazis.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_136152105" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136152105" />
    <a title="Pick Wick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2777425-pick-wick"><img alt="Pick Wick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406654893p2/2777425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136152105?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pick Wick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pick Wick" href="/user/show/2777425-pick-wick">Pick Wick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783462027242" href="/book/show/1967368.Der_Funke_Leben">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2777425-pick-wick?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2777425-pick-wick?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2777425-pick-wick?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2777425-pick-wick?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer136152105&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136152105" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11165587059410110850">Unlike other novels by Erich Maria Remarque, the end of this novel strangely almost feels like a happy end. But on second thought, it is as always a bitter sweet ending. So what is different? Why is the reader so relieved?<br/><br/>Probably because he/she is counting the pages bringing to the last blank pages where there would be no more of this insanity, unbelievable cruelty, this monstrousness out of human understanding. And when it finally comes, with the Allies taking the camp over, the reader, like t</span>
  <span id="freeText11165587059410110850" style="display:none">Unlike other novels by Erich Maria Remarque, the end of this novel strangely almost feels like a happy end. But on second thought, it is as always a bitter sweet ending. So what is different? Why is the reader so relieved?<br /><br />Probably because he/she is counting the pages bringing to the last blank pages where there would be no more of this insanity, unbelievable cruelty, this monstrousness out of human understanding. And when it finally comes, with the Allies taking the camp over, the reader, like the captives, also feels a liberation from the torture of the story.<br /><br />The story takes place in a concentration/labour camp (not an extermination camp) at the end of the war in the "too weak to work" section. We follow the life or rather the last attempts at surviving of Skeleton n°509. And the reader is soon realising that 509 is one of the lucky ones, the one that still has the courage and the will to fight. For he is surrounded by the "Muselmänner", those who can not be saved anymore, not because they have nothing to eat, nor because they are so ill they can not stand up anymore, nor even because they underwent the special treatment of the SS, but because they have lost the determination to fight.<br /><br />N°509 is the first one to allow himself to believe – which is one of the most dangerous things to do in camp – when he hears the first Allied bombs attacking the little German town he has been watching everyday through the barbed wires of this prison for ten years. Is it really possible that Germany can loose the War, … again? N°509 goes as far as to remember that he has a name and that he is not just a number.<br /><br />While they are giving life to the prisoners again, the bombs have exactly the opposite effect on the Nazis. Each attack, each time he witnesses the loss of something he earned cowardly, his shops, his family and finally his beloved garden, Obersturmbannführer Bruno Neubauer not only feels stronger and stronger that his adored Führer and all his puppets, like Weber, is loosing the War, but that all that he has done was perhaps not "ein Befehl", that all the suffering he caused was also his fault.<br /><br />As always, Remarque does not point out a hero and a bad guy. He clearly shows that all are losers in a war. While he certainly doesn't excuse Neubauer or Weber's crimes and motives – and makes them pay the big price for it –, he seems to say that they are as imprisoned in that camp as the prisoners, because even if the have wanted to escape and tried to put things right, they would not have been able to.<br /><br />The result is two human beings, the tortured and tormenter, lying on the same dirty floor, stained by tears, dejections, injustice and despair, looking at each other and fighting to be the last one to go. But there is also the cute couple, formed in the camp, looking at the setting sun surrounded by the remains of a house that used to be their only dream during their captivity, and reviving a spark of life.<br /><br />Remarque's writing is pristine, as always. I missed the wonderful humour that makes me usually laugh out loud, but laughing out loud during such a story would have been serious offence and quite indescent. The main characters are superb, especially 509 and Neubauer, and the supporting characters are brilliant – I have Leo Lebenthal (the dealer) or Ephraim Berger (the doctor, employed at the crematorium) in mind. The only criticism I would allow myself to utter is the choice of names. They are so similar that it is very difficult to remember who is who. It's really too bad to hesitate on a name over one page and wondering if it's the SS leader or the Communist revolutionary we are talking about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11165587059410110850" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

After its publication there was a great deal of criticism of Remarque for having written a novel about things he had never personally experienced. But I doubt that there are many literary treatments of this human tragedy that are as poignant or relevant. </span>

<div id="review_160205129" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160205129" />
    <a title="Anke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/104645-anke"><img alt="Anke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428076267p2/104645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/160205129?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anke" href="/user/show/104645-anke">Anke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160205129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9803521115782971286">Remarque is certainly not a light read, but a very readable one. One thing about him that I find most interesting is the way he shows human adaptability - the everyday life in situations most people would not even begin to imagine. Be it the front in WW I or longterm prisoners in a concentration camp, even the worst things do end up shaping some kind of routine around them, no matter how terrible, and humans seem to grow used to things that they would not be able to imagine in normal circumstanc</span>
  <span id="freeText9803521115782971286" style="display:none">Remarque is certainly not a light read, but a very readable one. One thing about him that I find most interesting is the way he shows human adaptability - the everyday life in situations most people would not even begin to imagine. Be it the front in WW I or longterm prisoners in a concentration camp, even the worst things do end up shaping some kind of routine around them, no matter how terrible, and humans seem to grow used to things that they would not be able to imagine in normal circumstances. It is deeply disturbing to read because of the way the main characters barely stop to think in the face of suffering or death as they're too used to it to notice (or as there is too much of it to think about it and still stay sane).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9803521115782971286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2955522535" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2955522535" />
    <a title="Ellie Midwood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33539108-ellie-midwood"><img alt="Ellie Midwood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473347829p2/33539108.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2955522535?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie Midwood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie Midwood" href="/user/show/33539108-ellie-midwood">Ellie Midwood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=wwii-fiction">wwii-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=nazi-germany">nazi-germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2955522535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14192317991483190720">A brilliant (as it always is with Remarque) novel dedicated - if I’m not mistaken - to the memory of his sister Elfriede who was executed after a trial at Nazi Germany's "People's Court" solely because of their family relation. It’s definitely much darker than his other novels (I’d say, even darker than “All Quiet on the Western Front” which certainly doesn’t lack any blood and gore) but it’s uplifting nevertheless; uplifting in the small acts of resistance that concentration camp inmates still </span>
  <span id="freeText14192317991483190720" style="display:none">A brilliant (as it always is with Remarque) novel dedicated - if I’m not mistaken - to the memory of his sister Elfriede who was executed after a trial at Nazi Germany's "People's Court" solely because of their family relation. It’s definitely much darker than his other novels (I’d say, even darker than “All Quiet on the Western Front” which certainly doesn’t lack any blood and gore) but it’s uplifting nevertheless; uplifting in the small acts of resistance that concentration camp inmates still find the strength in themselves to commit despite the constant threat from the SS and the outside world, which is literally going up in flames as more and more bombs start dropping on German land. The inter struggle to remain human even in the most inhumane circumstances; the willpower to sacrifice oneself for others; the ability to see hope where no hope can possibly be found - all these aspects make “Spark Of Life” a truly classic WWII novel.<br /><br />What I also highly appreciated was the fact that it’s not only a Holocaust novel but also a resistance novel, an anti-totalitarian regime novel (I’m not just talking here about Nazi Germany but the communist USSR as well); in general, it’s an anti-tyranny novel, pro-humanity novel that will touch you to the core with its final scenes (the ones, in which an American GI led the prisoners to their beds brought tears to my eyes, and it takes a lot for a book to make me emotional to such an extent). <br /><br />Exceptionally written and with an important message that we, as a society, could really use today, in our ultra-divided world. The novel is neither right nor left, it’s pro-human, and that’s what should be important. Highly recommended to all fans of the genre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14192317991483190720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_684998719" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/684998719" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18948588-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569598891p2/18948588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/684998719?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/18948588-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=four-star-novels">four-star-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=r-r">r-r</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer684998719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17782817686395643969"><i>Spark of Life</i> is a novel by Erich Maria Remarque, which appeared both in German (as Der Funke Leben) and in English in 1952. Remarque was a German author who created many works, with his best-known novel being<i> All Quiet on the Western Front</i>. One of the first things I wondered about Remarque was why his middle name was Maria, it seemed such an odd name to be given to a boy, then I read that he was born Erich Paul Remark and changed his middle name in memory of his mother and reverted to the earli</span>
  <span id="freeText17782817686395643969" style="display:none"><i>Spark of Life</i> is a novel by Erich Maria Remarque, which appeared both in German (as Der Funke Leben) and in English in 1952. Remarque was a German author who created many works, with his best-known novel being<i> All Quiet on the Western Front</i>. One of the first things I wondered about Remarque was why his middle name was Maria, it seemed such an odd name to be given to a boy, then I read that he was born Erich Paul Remark and changed his middle name in memory of his mother and reverted to the earlier spelling of the family name. Apparently the Nazis didn't like Remarque or his novels because on May 10, 1933 on the initiative of the Nazi propaganda minister, Joseph Goebbels, Remarque's works were banned and publicly burned. This got me wondering how many other books were burned but I haven't looked it up yet. The Nazis also claimed that he was a descendant of French Jews and that his real surname name was Kramer, a Jewish-sounding name, and his original name spelled backwards. I didn't look that up yet either to see if it's true, but it doesn't sound true and I don't think Kramer sounds any more Jewish than my name, but I can't think of any name that sounds particularly Jewish for that matter, so I'm moving on.<br /><br />The novel which I'm supposed to be talking about but haven't gotten around to yet is <i>Spark of Life</i> and is set in the fictional Mellern concentration/labor camp during the Holocaust. From the first sentence to the last I was hoping that the things that happened in the novel were worse than what happened in real life, because the things that happened in the novel were horrifying. Then after I finished the novel and reading about Remarque's life I read this:<br /><br /><i> "In 1938, Remarque's German citizenship was revoked and then in 1939 after he and his ex-wife were remarried to prevent her repatriation to Germany, they left Porto Ronco, Switzerland for the United States. They became naturalized citizens of the United States in 1947 In 1943, the government arrested his sister, Elfriede Scholz, who had stayed behind in Germany with her husband and two children. After a short trial in the "Volksgerichtshof" (Hitler's extra-constitutional "People's Court"), she was found guilty of "undermining morale" for stating that she considered the war lost. Court President Roland Freisler declared, "Your brother is unfortunately beyond our reach—you, however, will not escape us". Scholz was beheaded on December 16, 1943, and the cost of her prosecution, imprisonment and execution—495.80 Reichsmark—was billed to her sister Erna"</i>.<br /><br />I can't say anything about that except that it is as horrible as the things that happen in the book. And finally that brings me to the book which I hate to talk about, but I will. Right now this feels like the most horrifying book I've ever read. I hated it. The first sentence is <i>"Skeleton 509 slowly raised its skull and opened its eyes."</i> It never gets better for me, not much anyway. I think the only reason the ending seemed happy to me, other than the Americans arriving, which wasn't as happy as I thought it would be, was that it was ending, finally I would get out of the concentration camp. One of the things that really bothers me about this book, and there were many things that bothered me, was that I am supposed to forgive others, all others, no matter what. I have been able to do this, but if I were in Remarque's fictional Mellern concentration camp there are people that I just don't think I could forgive. That bothers me. A lot. Oh well, on to the story.<br /><br />The story takes place in the Mellern concentration camp, it is a labor camp which I learned is not the same thing as an extermination camp; at the end, or almost the end of the war. Most of the characters in the novel are in the "Small" camp officially called the "Mercy" camp which is the "too weak to work" section. These people were there to die and we are told "most of them did quickly". There was however, a small, tough group in Barrack 22 that called themselves the "Veterans". Most of the book is about these veterans. Our main character seems to be Skeleton 509 but he's not the only "main" character for me. There are many other prisoners and Nazis who have stories in the book. There is Karel, a boy from Czechoslovakia who is there as a political prisoner. He is eleven years old. Dr. Ephraim Berger has been there twelve years because he had a Nazi patient who had syphilis, even though another doctor treated him Berger was jailed just for knowing. I guess Nazis couldn't have syphilis, I'll have to look that up too. Bucher had been there seven years because his father was an editor of a Social Democratic newspaper, then there were Jews and some were criminals. The actual criminals seemed to be treated the best. 509's name we don't find out until half way through the book, he is Friedrich Koller, a former journalist who refused to divulge names of persons sought by the Gestapo, he's been there ten years. Even after we know his name we rarely hear it, he is always 509.<br /><br />Then we have the Commandant, Obersturmbannfuhrer (good luck with that word) Neubauer, he loved his wife and daughter, his rabbits, chickens, pigs and garden, his businesses and his career. He doesn't seem to see anything wrong with the things he has done. When he speeds to town after the first bombing to check on his office building we are told that he bought the building for 5000 marks. The Jewish owner had wanted a hundred thousand marks but after two weeks in a concentration camp had been glad to sell it to Neubauer for what he offered:<br /><br /> <i>"A Legal sale. Of his own free will. Attested by the notary. The fact that Max Blank had accidentally fallen down in the camp, lost an eye, broken an arm, and otherwise hurt himself had been a regrettable incident."</i><br /><br />Then there is this conversation between Neubauer and Weber the SS camp leader:<br /><br /><i>"Neubauer stood by the window and looked out. 'There's also a request come in about the food supplies. We're expected to economize. How can this be done?"<br />"Hand out less food," answered Weber laconically.<br />"That's possible only up to a certain point. If the people collapse they can't work any more."<br />"We can save on the Small camp. It's full of useless mouths. Who dies no longer eats."<br />Neubauer nodded. "All the same, you know my motto: Always humane, as long as possible. Of course when it's no longer possible -"</i><br /><br />Yes, Neubauer who is always making excuses for the horrible things he does:<br /><br /><i>"He could have gotten more out of life if only he hadn't been such a faithful family man. Faithful - well, as good as faithful, one could say. Really faithful if you considered what he could have had. Those few times! The widow - that hardly counted. A few years ago there'd been a red-haired woman who had come to rescue her husband from the camp-the things she had done in her distress! Actually, the husband had died long before. Naturally, she didn't know that. It had been a gay evening. Later, of course, when she received the cigar box with the ashes, she had behaved like an idiot. All her own fault she'd gotten locked up. An Obersturmbannfuhrer couldn't allow himself to be spat at."</i><br /><br />We have this when an unfortunate rabbit wonders into this place:<br /><br /><i>The hare raised itself and sniffed. At this moment one of the dozing SS guard must have seen it. "Edgar! Man alive! A long-ear!" he yelled. "At it!"<br />A few shots rang out. Earth flew up. In long bounds, the hare leapt away. "You see," said 509, "they can only hit prisoners from the closest range. For that they then get a furlough and decorations."<br />Lebenthal sighed and stared after the hare.</i><br /><br />I had more things marked, but I can't take any more so I'll quit. I'm almost done now. I thought it was interesting that the town of Mellern in the novel was based on Remarque's hometown of Osnabruck. Supposedly it is an exact replica of Osnabruck even down to the street names and town layout, however there was never a concentration camp at Osnabruck. I'm tired of thinking of this book. I am also puzzled as to how many stars to give it. I give five stars to novels I can barely live without. I give four stars to novels I love, novels that make me feel good. But three stars seems to low, three stars I liked but may have to read my review to remind myself what it was about after some time has passed. I won't forget this book. I can't tell you to read it, you'll have to decide that for yourself this time. On to the next book. Happy reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17782817686395643969" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5958401-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546517338p2/5958401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/459856756?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/5958401-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2446590782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer629671631671714646">This has been by far one of the worst reading experiences I've had in a while. It took me a great deal of will to read it through, the only thing that kept me going was my inability to quit on books, even when they are awfully boring. It pains me to part with Remarque, as we've had a wonderful run so far, but I won't be buying one of his books any time soon. I'll give it a couple of years for the painful memory to subside. The plot. Was there any really? It seemed like a never-ending ramble abou</span>
  <span id="freeText629671631671714646" style="display:none">This has been by far one of the worst reading experiences I've had in a while. It took me a great deal of will to read it through, the only thing that kept me going was my inability to quit on books, even when they are awfully boring. It pains me to part with Remarque, as we've had a wonderful run so far, but I won't be buying one of his books any time soon. I'll give it a couple of years for the painful memory to subside. The plot. Was there any really? It seemed like a never-ending ramble about camp daily life and meaningless discussions amongst inmates. I get it, this was probably the message the author wanted to convey, but come on, put a twist to it, spin it around somehow, we are falling asleep here. Also, after having a nightmare where I was stepping on corpses while running from bombings I decided that I might have to put raw history fiction aside for a while and read something more uplifting. Recommended to people who enjoy boring stories. </span>
  <a data-text-id="629671631671714646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1272318424" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1272318424" />
    <a title="Георги Кафадаров" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40656022"><img alt="Георги Кафадаров" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428750778p2/40656022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1272318424?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Георги Кафадаров" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Георги Кафадаров" href="/user/show/40656022">Георги Кафадаров</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/15986886">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40656022?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1272318424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16885240743309605486"> Despair and hope.Those are the two conflicting themes in the book "Spark of life".The book is set in Mellern concentration camp in Nazi Germany towards the end of the Second World War.509 is the main charachter and one of the longest serving inmates in the camp.He himself prefers to be called "509",a number given by the camp authority,because his real name reminds him of his life as a free man.The others are Joseph Bucher (the son of a left-wing editor), Ruth Holland (Bucher’s girlfriend), Old </span>
  <span id="freeText16885240743309605486" style="display:none"> Despair and hope.Those are the two conflicting themes in the book "Spark of life".The book is set in Mellern concentration camp in Nazi Germany towards the end of the Second World War.509 is the main charachter and one of the longest serving inmates in the camp.He himself prefers to be called "509",a number given by the camp authority,because his real name reminds him of his life as a free man.The others are Joseph Bucher (the son of a left-wing editor), Ruth Holland (Bucher’s girlfriend), Old Ahasver (an aged survivor of several camps), Leo Lebenthal (a former businessman), and Karel, a Czechoslovakian boy whose parents have died in Nazi gas chambers.The main antagonists are the SS officers Neubauer and Weber who are in charge of the camp and the extermination of the prisoners.<br /> Life in the camp is cruel and unforgiving.One wrong step could cost your life.The prisoners use everything they can to their advantage,despite being barely alive, looking like walking skeletons.Many of them succumb to the hunger and despair and are driven insane by it.<br /> The novel's momentum is derived from the realization of the prisoners that the war is nearly over-the near town is bombed by the allies,the SS guards<br />are fewer and more lenient towards the prisoners.This "awakens" them and they start plotting a resistance ,sensing the SS would wipe them out before the allies forces arrive.In the end as the nazis try to purge the captives, 509 manages to kill Weber, but also dies in the process.The rest of the characters<br />live proving there is still life and hope among the death and despair that haunt Mellern concentration camp.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="16885240743309605486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3133138198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3133138198" />
    <a title="Reading Cat " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33407663-reading-cat"><img alt="Reading Cat " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578331044p2/33407663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3133138198?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reading Cat " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reading Cat " href="/user/show/33407663-reading-cat">Reading Cat </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33407663-reading-cat?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33407663-reading-cat?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33407663-reading-cat?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3133138198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3514400815305182467">After *Reader, Come Home*, I performed a similar experiment to Wolf--I wanted to read a book that I had read before, and see if/how I could feel the same power. I haven't read this novel since before I got sick, and I'd forgotten almost everything about it except that I had loved it. I didn't remember who lived, who died, just an incredible sense of tragic bleakness. <br/><br/>I concentrated reading slowly, forcing myself to go back and re-read passages I found myself skimming over. Remarque is great for </span>
  <span id="freeText3514400815305182467" style="display:none">After *Reader, Come Home*, I performed a similar experiment to Wolf--I wanted to read a book that I had read before, and see if/how I could feel the same power. I haven't read this novel since before I got sick, and I'd forgotten almost everything about it except that I had loved it. I didn't remember who lived, who died, just an incredible sense of tragic bleakness. <br /><br />I concentrated reading slowly, forcing myself to go back and re-read passages I found myself skimming over. Remarque is great for this, because he has alternates between stripped down, almost Hemingway-esque suggestive prose (you have to read into it quite a bit) and these flights of poetry that just want to immerse you. I re-read those passages until they could work that magic on me. <br /><br />I still love this book. I still cried my face off. THIS, I think, is what I've been wanting all last year when I tried to read all that historical fiction (that all turned out to be terrible). Despite the fact that Remarque has never been a concentration camp prisoner, he somehow has an authenticity, a psychological realness, that these latter day 21st century novelists lack, as they write surrounded by food and safety. It's no secret I love Remarque and have read literally every book of his I've ever been able to put my hands on. None are bad. Some better than others, and this, I really think, is his best work, better than All Quiet on the Western Front. <br /><br />It's fascinating to watch his Nazis wriggle with morality, try to paint themselves as good guys, justified guys, brave guys...except the Weber, who seems to reveal himself as a psychopath. He's not a Nazi. He's not a Socialist. He'll do and be whatever is needed that he can be the vicious thug he is. We see the full range here, from die hards like Steinbrenner, social climbers, thieves and cowards. <br /><br />And in the camp prisoners, we see courage and cowardice, strength and weakness but all with extreme sensitivity. All the Nazis are bad, but bad in different ways--they're not all a caricature of a robotic brainwashed monster. All the camp prisoners are good, but again, in different ways. Their conversations are vital: how do you survive? How can you move on past something like what they've endured? What place is revenge? What justice CAN be done? <br /><br />Really, this should be a book that every human being needs to read. Just...everyone. Because it's not about 'Nazis' and it's not about an evil ideology. It is about what makes us human and how we can recover hope. And that is something that all of us need in our lives. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3514400815305182467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2730816789" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2730816789" />
    <a title="Dirk Langeveld" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75683833-dirk-langeveld"><img alt="Dirk Langeveld" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514561944p2/75683833.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2730816789?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dirk Langeveld" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dirk Langeveld" href="/user/show/75683833-dirk-langeveld">Dirk Langeveld</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2730816789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4048605493865340735">Erich Maria Remarque brings the same vivid imagery and haunting prose to this novel that was so apparent in his classic "All Quiet on the Western Front." The story is set in the waning days of World War II, when a bombing attack on a sleepy German town near a concentration camp inspires the prisoners to dare to hope that they'll survive the conflict.<br/><br/>Remarque delivers an unflinching look at the cruelty of war, from the tortures of the SS to the squalid conditions the prisoners endure to the destr</span>
  <span id="freeText4048605493865340735" style="display:none">Erich Maria Remarque brings the same vivid imagery and haunting prose to this novel that was so apparent in his classic "All Quiet on the Western Front." The story is set in the waning days of World War II, when a bombing attack on a sleepy German town near a concentration camp inspires the prisoners to dare to hope that they'll survive the conflict.<br /><br />Remarque delivers an unflinching look at the cruelty of war, from the tortures of the SS to the squalid conditions the prisoners endure to the destruction rained down on civilians. There's an ongoing tension throughout the book; the reader keenly feels the same anxiety of the prisoners as they worry whether they will be shipped off to the gas chambers, die of illness, or be slaughtered outright before the liberators arrive. "Spark of Life" explores other themes as well, including how some Nazis attempt to justify their actions, the question of how thoroughly the Nazi regime can be uprooted when it has poisoned a generation of youth, and the uncertainty the prisoners will face if they can escape their ordeal.<br /><br />It can be a little tough to keep track of the characters in the novel, since it encompasses several prisoners as well as their Nazi captors. But this is the type of book that rewards multiple readings, and is sure to appeal to anyone who found themselves moved by Remarque's earlier work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4048605493865340735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3214734113" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5650195003312141267">This book... Oh, my God this book... <br/>I am shattered into little pieces. I loved it with every part of my heart. The whole plot was so perfectly written and told that I could not do anything else but be intimidated to read further and understand what will happen next. Such a heart felt story, and indeed a very powerful one. <br/>The scenes were harsh most of the times, but then we must expect that because this book is set in a concentration camp during World War II. It deals a lot with death, but what</span>
  <span id="freeText5650195003312141267" style="display:none">This book... Oh, my God this book... <br />I am shattered into little pieces. I loved it with every part of my heart. The whole plot was so perfectly written and told that I could not do anything else but be intimidated to read further and understand what will happen next. Such a heart felt story, and indeed a very powerful one. <br />The scenes were harsh most of the times, but then we must expect that because this book is set in a concentration camp during World War II. It deals a lot with death, but what stands out the most is the will of the prisoners to survive. To survive the horrific torture and massacre they faced every day from the terrible SS army of Nationalsocialists. The burning, all kinds of illnesses and all kinds of savagery is portrayed in this book, so for someone looking forward to read this masterpiece should be aware of its damageful impact it may have in one's mind. <br />However I wasn't traumatized by these scenes at all and it's probably because I love a good death scene be it in movies or books. But setting this aside, this novel of historical fiction has made me fall in love even more with this genre in general. I just am really interested to know how can authors who have never been in concentration camps or any other part of a history events and places describe it so majesticly well. I am dying to see if there is any other book of Remark in the library, because he might just turn out to be one of my favorite authors (of course, I will make such decisions after I've read any of his other books).</span>
  <a data-text-id="5650195003312141267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2284649509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17785436866321584309">Spark of Life tells the story of the survival, not of human beings, but humanity itself, which propagates in small acts of virtue and altruism--saving a crust of bread for an ailing prisoner, for instance. Remarque also does an amazingly astute job of plumbing the psychology of denial, the mindset that is required for human beings to treat other human beings as disposable animals. Although it is a novel, and written by a man who spent WWII not in the camps but in exile, many of its scenes are tr</span>
  <span id="freeText17785436866321584309" style="display:none">Spark of Life tells the story of the survival, not of human beings, but humanity itself, which propagates in small acts of virtue and altruism--saving a crust of bread for an ailing prisoner, for instance. Remarque also does an amazingly astute job of plumbing the psychology of denial, the mindset that is required for human beings to treat other human beings as disposable animals. Although it is a novel, and written by a man who spent WWII not in the camps but in exile, many of its scenes are tragically familiar when compared to the writings of Elie Wiesel and Viktor Frankl.</span>

    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2BBRwr/qDV+IJhFA6dJKhVKN71Rq+N0BS955pCUn8tHNqUQEiSEu4iGLun+TX6PNzRuu7+ShQyr3UyjT8skNBg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AzIOwMwvXMaFrijJjO3czmfjmhTPC+Tj0iYyxp0iHjgWixsG+uR/eywDg/b2YDWG+HXbr0FSeshuq2OxSszh7w==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Christians are rare in pagan, hedonistic Rome, and suffer great persecution;\n\nPetronius, uncle to Vinicius, an elegant, witty courtier who scoffs at love and religion but finds his nephew\'s passion charming; and\n\nNero himself, enemy of all Christians, a despotic emperor who plunges Rome deeper and deeper into depravity. The decadence of his banquets is staggering; and even worse, his mad laughter is heard echoing in the amphitheater as gladiators duel to the death.\n\nAs Nero\'s appalling plans for the Christians become ever clearer, time appears to be running out for the young lovers. Vinicius must come to understand the true meaning of Ligia\'s religion before it is too late.\n\nGrand in scope and ambition, Quo Vadis explores the themes of love, desire and profound moral courage. Lavish descriptions, vivid dialogue and brilliantly drawn characters make this one of the world\'s greatest epics.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_307794'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/307794.Jennie_Gerhardt?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Jennie Gerhardt<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/8987.Theodore_Dreiser?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Theodore Dreiser<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.05 avg rating &mdash; 2,682 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1911\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8750129615156256468\">Jennie Gerhardt was Theodore Dreiser\'s second novel and his first true commercial success. Today it is generally regarded as one of his three best novels, along with Sister Carrie and An American Tragedy. But the text of Jennie Gerhardt heretofore kn<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8750129615156256468\" style=\"display:none\">Jennie Gerhardt was Theodore Dreiser\'s second novel and his first true commercial success. Today it is generally regarded as one of his three best novels, along with Sister Carrie and An American Tragedy. But the text of Jennie Gerhardt heretofore known to readers is quite different from the text as Dreiser originally wrote it. In the tradition of the University of Pennsylvania Dreiser Edition, James L. W. West III has recaptured the text as it was originally written, restoring it to its complete, unexpurgated form. As submitted to Harper and Brothers in 1911, Jennie Gerhardt was a powerful study of a woman tragically compromised by birth and fate. Harpers agreed to publish the book but was nervous about its subject matter and moral stance. Jennie has an illegitimate child by one man and lives out of wedlock with another - but Dreiser does not condemn her for her behavior. As a requirement for publication, Harpers insisted on cutting and revising the text. Although Dreiser fought against many of the cuts and succeeded in restoring some material, Harpers shortened the text by 16,000 words and completely revised its style and tone. These changes ultimately transformed Jennie Gerhardt from a blunt, carefully documented work of social realism to a touching love story merely set against a social background. Passages critical of organized religion and of the institution of marriage were reduced and altered. Perhaps most important, Jennie\'s point of view - her innate romantic mysticism - was largely edited out of the text. As a consequence, the central dialectic of the novel was skewed and the narrative thrown out of balance.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8750129615156256468\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));;       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque">About Erich Maria Remarque</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1207351165p3/4116.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12480429537012991783">Erich Maria Remarque (pen name of Erich Paul Remark) is one of the best known and most widely read authors of German literature in the twentieth century. <br/><br/>Remarque's biography is essentially marked and his writing fundamentally influenced by German history of the twentieth century: Childhood and youth in imperial Osnabrück, World War I, the Weimar Republic, and most of all his exile in Switzerland </span>
  <span id="freeText12480429537012991783" style="display:none">Erich Maria Remarque (pen name of Erich Paul Remark) is one of the best known and most widely read authors of German literature in the twentieth century. <br /><br />Remarque's biography is essentially marked and his writing fundamentally influenced by German history of the twentieth century: Childhood and youth in imperial Osnabrück, World War I, the Weimar Republic, and most of all his exile in Switzerland and the United States. With the novel All Quiet On the Western Front, first published in 1929, Remarque attained world-wide recognition continuing today. <br /><br />Examples of his other novels also internationally published are: The Road Back (1931), Three Comrades (1936, 38), Arch of Triumph (1945), The Black Obelisk (1956), and Night in Lisbon (1962). <br /><br />Remarque's novels have been translated in more than fifty languages; globally the total edition comes up to several million copies. <br /><br />The complete works of Remarque are both highly interrelated with his Osnabrück background and speaking thematically of a critical examination of German history, whereby the preservation of human dignity and humanity in times of oppression, terror and war always was at the forefront of his literary creation. <br /><br />AKA:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17900575._________________" title="Έριχ Μαρία Ρεμάρκ" rel="nofollow noopener">Έριχ Μαρία Ρεμάρκ</a> (Greek)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8106923._________________" title="Эрих Мария Ремарк" rel="nofollow noopener">Эрих Мария Ремарк</a> (Russian)</span>
  <a data-text-id="12480429537012991783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
